Years ago I did what you did and never regretted it!! I built by hand a 1000 square foot log home in the CLEAR WATER mountains in Idaho. It took me four summers to cut and form up the floor joist, wall logs rafters, purlins and close everything in and then start work on the inside!! NO plywood, particle board or sheet rock was used anywhere!! Even the interior walls were log!! YES lots of work period!! I am only about 5 min into your video and feel so compelled to express a couple things if I may... First; I totally feel Jake in his expression of his feelings at this point. A responsible husband and father will natural want to make every effort to provide what he believes his family needs. His conflict is valid. But he knows this already. If it helps; I commend and support anyone's decision to have a home birth. I don't know when a childbirth became a hospital emergency by itself for society, but birthing a child is not an emergency. Society has trained people to feel like they can't do it without the help of the medical industry. This is false and promotes  incapability. I know this for a few reasons. I'll mention that I have worked at a hospital for a good number of years, but also I have twice now participated in my own children's birth, at home. I've seen plenty of births at the hospital and in my experience, a byproduct of societies' exchange of home births for hospital births is that the father is often completely left out of the experience. The father's role has become nothing more than either financial alone, or a "hand holder" at best. But mostly inconsequential, when it comes to the actual childbirth... This breaks my heart for father's. Father's have a drive to help installed in their DNA. We're problem solvers. When it comes to childbirth, one of the most significant moments in a father's life, in this society, men/father's are left out. I also cannot measure the impact this may have on his children... This society won't reach out to father's and provide them this information and I had to earn mine. If more father's took some time to learn about what they can do and how they can be useful for childbirth, including providing counter pressure during contractions, the stages of birth and what can be expected from the mother during times like the "transition period" etc., more father's would be far more involved, attached, and invested in the experience and therefore far more rewarded... It is work. Very involved both physically and emotionally. To push on your wife's hips while you know she's in pain and crying, telling  you that she needs help and can't do this anymore and you have to navigate this and figure it all out... The accomplishment in this regard is unimaginable for father's who have never done it. This who have hold these experiences close. Don't get me wrong, fatherhood in any respect can be very rewarding. But if I could say anything to all expecting father's it would be this, and that you are far more valuable and needed than you, your wife, and society knows, tells you, and gives you credit for. There is far more you can do to benefit your wife, your children and yourself in this experience of you want it. Having done it, and hearing you express your feelings, I believe you both want it. Don't let fear guide you. It's difficult when it comes to the safety of your wife and children, I know. You're going to feel a kind of anxiety that you probably haven't felt before, but just know that a woman's body is amazing and does what it needs to do naturally and you as a father are needed and can be such a valuable help in this process. Childbirth is not an emergency. Having a trained midwife is a great thing. Learn all you can about their experiences and what they've seen others do that are helpful.  Anyway, sorry for the long comment and I hope it not only makes sense but that I've been able to express the message that maybe you've needed. Maybe I'm being pretentious and if so, a apologize, that's not my intention. Maybe when this is complete for you, you'll feel the same way about trying to help other father's...  Reach out if you have questions or if things I've said need clarity - I'm sure you have plenty of ppl around you that are willing to help. Just know that I guess, I'm another one and would gladly help how I can. What a fantastic opportunity you both have and I'm excited for you both!

Hi Nicolle and Jake! I've been watching your videos for a while now and really love following your off grid journey. I am a doula and birthworker in Upstate NY. I know you probably know, it seems like you have a wonderful midwife and you've been doing so much research on your own, but I couldn't help but mention it - itching in the third trimester can sometimes mean simply that your skin is stretching out and accommodating the baby like you mentioned. I would keep an eye on that and maybe log how often you feel itchy because it can also indicate a liver condition, cholestasis. Like I mentioned, it's usually just stretching of the skin but I would definitely let your midwife know, if you haven't already, and keep an eye on it.   Sending love and and healthy vibes to you for your upcoming labor and delivery! I know it will be everything you imagined and more! â¤

Jake, your thoughts, although natural and common, are actually irrational and the usual fears and anxieties of new fathers.  There is a big change coming and it is something you have not been privy to up till now.  Nicole, being in the job already for 7 months, has already got a genetically detailed blueprint plan laid out in her head and her heart and she has probably less fears of the future than you do.  For us men, our journey doesn't start until we lay eyes on our newborn child.  Once that moment occurs, the whole world changes and all things that were important before are no longer important.  And all the insignificant before, suddenly become significant.  Jake, you are also frightened that as a new father you won't be able to keep up the level of work required to keep your home in the forest alive and viable.  You fear something happening to your child and then having to somehow get them to safety for medical attention. Well, didn't you both take all this into consideration prior to moving out there?  Surely you did, because you clearly planned to have children.  So if that is the case, then follow your original plans.  You have a good solid base built up for your home now.  It will not degrade in the time you are away.  Sure you'll have some work getting back to where you were, because you'll be behind with planting, but maybe the next thing you build is a large greenhouse so you can actually grow somethings out of season.  Take a breath Jake. Do what is necessary for your child to come into this world safely, which means returning to civilization.  Then return as soon as the baby is able to travel.  Ensure that you have all things ready at home for the baby's arrival, before leaving.  You're an organised man.  So, get organised and follow the numbers.  Do this and your anxiety will lesson.  Have faith.  I am not a follower of the pagan religion you follow, but as a Christian I know that faith is a very powerful force.  You have faith in your home, the ground it sits on, the ability for it to grow food, your ability to build and create, your wife's ability to do all the things you cannot.  That is what you call ying and yang I think.  I just call it the laws of nature and balance.  Faith drives it all.  Without faith in each other, in yourself, and in God, we have nothing.   If Nicole has decided that she cannot live in that world with a new born, then I guess you have a problem. That is something you will both have to work out and decide when you can return.  If that is not the case, then there is no problem.  There is only delays in your plans, because they have been deliberately put on hold for the wonderful child that God has given you.  Rejoice and hold the faith!

Hey Jake ; I been hunting rock scallops for years from the coast of california on scuba and one tool you need is just a flat metal prybar to loosen the scallop from the rock and you can also just take the meat and inner if you use a cake spatula ,so you just scrape the very top or bottom to loosen the white muscle from the shell  from both sides and it is a perfect clean to not waste any bit at all , pulling it by force you loose pieces or chunks and the scallop meat gets some waste is the bad part; if taken the whole shell to clean at home then it is even easier to clean it with the $2 cake spatula (we call it the martha steward special ,they are long and flexible and contours to the inside of the shell to clean out the meat ) best tool to clean big scallops but many times if it does not fit thru the closed mouth then you need a good knife to brake the back black hinge first ,then scrape with the flexible spatula top and bottom to get all the meat... cheers and good luck with the baby , enjoying all your videos with your lovely girl !

Love you guys and happy New Year! But hey, don't move your scallops around in the pan. The browning you'll get my not moving them until it's time to flip is called the maillard reaction, and it will provide better depth of flavor and texture. Flip them when you start seeing tiny cracks in the browning coming up the sides. Keep the veggies away from the scallops while you cook them also, as the residual steam from the veggies toughens them up.
